if you want to compare cancel culture to historical phenomena, the most appropriate ones i can think of are the jacquerie or the revolution of 1525: peasants who, after being treated like shit for several centuries, formed angry mobs to inflict misery on the aristocracy.

 and thus cancel culture carries value in that sense, because it makes the cost of empathising only with those close to you much higher.
Of course, it is scary and open to abuse and I‚Äôd hate to face it. But cancel culture has political and intellectual value because of the very real, material discomfort it causes.
Cancel culture forces you to have guilt about sneakily listening to an old song or reading an old post by someone problematic. It forces you to contend with a more realistic morality.
Cancel culture forces us to realise that any sort of support or approval in a capitalist society leads to material benefits for those involved. A positive tweet is monetisable, as is a Facebook like, as is the decision to stream a song.
I don‚Äôt disagree entirely with such assessments. But one thing that I keep coming back to is that cancel culture forces us to consider our consumption as a consequence.
